Objective

Each participant will

individually design, build, and pitch

an AI-driven solution that addresses a

real-world problem

in a chosen domain (business, industry, public sector, sustainability, etc.).

What they will deliver by May 2026

A

working AI prototype

(using

Langdock

, Claude  Enterprise, N8N  or equivalent tools

)

A

clear problem–solution narrative

A

business and technical justification

A

final investor / executive-style pitch

Key Principle

This is

not a demo for technology’s sake

— it’s about

solving the right problem with the right AI approach

.

Your Project

From Problem Discovery to AI-Powered Prototype

Clearly articulated

pain point or opportunity

or

## Slide 15

🧠 Human Brain

(Nature’s Supercomputer)

The human brain has

~86 BILLION neurons

Each neuron can connect to

up to 10,000 other neurons

That’s

over 100 TRILLION connections

🤯

Brain uses only

about 20 watts of energy

👉 less than a light bulb!

Humans can:

learn from

one example

feel emotions

imagine things that don’t exist

think creatively

🤖 Artificial Intelligence

(Man-made Brain)

AI models have

hundreds of BILLIONS of artificial neurons

Need

millions or billions of examples

to learn

Training AI can use

as much electricity as thousands of homes

AI can:

calculate

millions of answers per second

beat humans at chess, Go, and video games

analyze huge amounts of data very fast

The human brain is

the smartest

,

most efficient computer

ever made —

AI is powerful, but it’s only

a tool

inspired

by us

.

## Slide 16

What is Intelligence ?

Dimension

Human Intelligence (Gold Standard)

Artificial Intelligence (Today)

Learning

Learns from very few examples (one-shot)

Requires massive labeled datasets

Reasoning

Abstract, analogical, causal

Statistical, correlational

Pattern Recognition

Understands meaning, emotion, social cues

Matches narrow patterns extremely well

Creativity

Generates novel ideas and solutions

Optimizes within predefined rules

Adaptation

Transfers knowledge across domains

Task-specific, poor transfer

Common Sense

Intuitive understanding of the world

Frequently fails at simple reasoning

Context

Meaning changes with situation

Fragile to small input changes

Emotional Intelligence

Empathy, social awareness

None

Self-Awareness

Reflects on its own thinking

No awareness of itself or limits

## Slide 27

The History of Artificial Intelligence

From a Thought Experiment to a Force That May Redefine Humanity

1950 — One Question That Changed History

In a world of vacuum tubes and war-torn cities,

Alan Turing

asks a forbidden question:

“Can machines think?”

Not

calculate

.

Not

compute

.

Think.

That question launches a chain reaction humanity has never been able to stop.

## Slide 28

The History of Artificial Intelligence

From a Thought Experiment to a Force That May Redefine Humanity

1956 — Extreme Optimism, Extreme Failure

At the

Dartmouth Conference

, scientists boldly declare:

“Human-level intelligence will be solved in 20 years.”

They believe intelligence is just

logic + rules

.

Reality responds brutally.

Machines collapse outside toy problems

No intuition

No common sense

No understanding

Funding disappears. Careers end.

AI Winters

begin.

First lesson:

Intelligence is not explicit knowledge. It’s implicit.

## Slide 29

The History of Artificial Intelligence

From a Thought Experiment to a Force That May Redefine Humanity

1997 — Humanity Loses… and Doesn’t Notice

IBM

’s

Deep Blue

defeats world champion

Garry Kasparov

.

The world panics — then laughs.

Why?

Because Deep Blue understands

nothing

.

It’s force, not intelligence.

Humans feel safe again.

They shouldn’t have.

## Slide 30

The History of Artificial Intelligence

From a Thought Experiment to a Force That May Redefine Humanity

2012 — The Accident That Changed Everything

No grand theory.

No philosophical breakthrough.

Just:

Massive data

GPUs

Neural networks

Machines stop following instructions.

They start

learning from the world

.

Image recognition improves

more in 2 years than in the previous 40

.

This is the real birth of modern AI —

and almost nobody notices.

## Slide 31

The History of Artificial Intelligence

From a Thought Experiment to a Force That May Redefine Humanity

2016 — The Moment AI Became Alien

DeepMind

’s

AlphaGo

defeats

Lee Sedol

.

But that’s not the moment.

The moment is

Move 37

.

A move:

No human would play

Experts call a mistake

Turns out to be

brilliant

For the first time, AI doesn’t imitate humans.

It invents knowledge humans don’t have.

## Slide 32

The History of Artificial Intelligence

From a Thought Experiment to a Force That May Redefine Humanity

2020s — Intelligence Goes Public

Foundation models arrive.

One system learns:

Language

Code

Images

Reasoning

Creativity

Tools like

ChatGPT

escape research labs and enter society.

For the first time:

AI is not a tool. It’s a collaborator.

## Slide 34

🏥 HEALTHCARE & MEDICINE

## Slide 35

Artificial Intelligence Applications

Across All Domains

🏥 HEALTHCARE & MEDICINE

Current AI Applications

1. Medical Imaging Diagnosis

AI models detecting cancer, fractures, and anomalies in X-rays, MRIs, and CT scans

Examples: Google DeepMind's breast cancer detection (outperforms radiologists),

PathAI

for pathology analysis

Technology: Convolutional Neural Networks (CNNs), Vision Transformers

2. Drug Discovery

ML accelerating molecule design and predicting drug-target interactions

Examples: AlphaFold for protein structure prediction,

Insilico

Medicine discovering novel compounds

Reduces drug discovery time from 10+ years to 2-3 years

3. Clinical Decision Support

AI assisting with diagnosis and treatment recommendations

Examples: IBM Watson Health, Babylon Health symptom checker

Natural Language Processing analyzing medical records

## Slide 36

Artificial Intelligence Applications

Across All Domains

🏥 HEALTHCARE & MEDICINE

Innovation Gaps & Opportunities

Rare Diseases

- Only 5-7% of 7,000+ rare diseases have treatments; AI models are lacking

Healthcare Disparities

- Most models trained on Western populations, failing for diverse ethnicities

Mental Health Crisis

- Need accessible, personalized interventions at scale

Low-Resource Languages

- Medical knowledge unavailable in most languages

Explainability

- Black-box models lack clinical trust and regulatory approval

## Slide 59

Three Stages of AI Evolution:

The Roadmap

Stage

ANI — Narrow Intelligence (Now)

AGI — General Intelligence (Next)

ASI — Superintelligence (Beyond)

Definition

AI specialized in a single task

AI with human-level intelligence across all tasks

AI vastly smarter than humans in every domain

Timeframe

1950s – Present (2025)

~2030–2070 (highly uncertain)

Unknown — could follow AGI rapidly

Capability

Superhuman at narrow tasks

Human-level across all cognitive domains

Beyond human comprehension

Learning

No transfer between tasks

Learns & transfers like humans

Recursive self-improvement

Examples

ChatGPT, AlphaGo, Autopilot, recommenders

None yet

Purely hypothetical

Economic Impact

Massive, already visible

Total economic & social transformation

Civilization-scale consequences

Risk Level

Low

Moderate → High

Extreme

Core Limitation / Question

Zero generalization

Alignment & speed of arrival

Can humans retain control?

ANI → AGI:

Requires breakthroughs in

reasoning, common sense, and transfer learning

AGI → ASI:

Could be

gradual or explosive

— impossible to predict

## Slide 6

Generative AI, Agentic AI in 4 Years…

Hype, FOMO but Value!

## Slide 7

Definition:

Generative AI refers to artificial intelligence systems that can create new content—text, images, audio, video, code, or data—that resembles human-created content. Unlike traditional AI that classifies or predicts, Generative AI produces original outputs.

The Fundamental Shift:

Traditional AI (Discriminative):

Task:

Classification and prediction

Example:

Is this email spam or not spam?

Output:

Label, category, or prediction

Function:

Input → Analysis → Classification

Business Use:

Fraud detection, recommendation systems, diagnostics

Generative AI:

Task:

Content creation and generation

Example:

Write an email in professional tone

Output:

New, original content

Function:

Prompt → Creation → Novel Output

Business Use:

Content creation, code generation, design, automation

Generative AI :

Overview

## Slide 10

Generative AI :

Major Categories and Players

1. Text Generation (Large Language Models - LLMs)

Players: OpenAI (GPT-5, GPT-4o), Anthropic (Claude), Google (Gemini), Meta (Llama), Mistral, Cohere

Use Cases: Writing, analysis, coding, customer service, research synthesis, translation

Market Leaders: GPT-5 (most capable), Claude (longest context), Gemini (multimodal)

2. Image Generation

Players: Midjourney, DALL-E 3, Stable Diffusion, Adobe Firefly, Ideogram

Use Cases: Marketing visuals, product design, concept art, social media content, prototyping

Technology: Primarily diffusion models

3. Video Generation

Players: Runway (Gen-3), Pika, Sora (OpenAI - limited release),

Synthesia

(avatar-based)

Use Cases: Advertisements, training videos, content creation, explainer videos

Current State: Rapidly evolving, 3-60 second clips typical

## Slide 11

Generative AI :

Major Categories and Players

4. Audio/Voice

Players:

ElevenLabs

(voice cloning), OpenAI Whisper (transcription), Descript (editing)

Use Cases: Voiceovers, podcasts, transcription, dubbing, accessibility

5. Code Generation

Players: GitHub Copilot, Cursor,

Replit

AI, Amazon

CodeWhisperer

,

Tabnine

Use Cases: Software development, automation scripts, debugging, documentation

## Slide 14

Layer 1: Infrastructure (Compute)

NVIDIA GPUs, Google TPUs, cloud providers

Cost: Billions in capital investment

Players: NVIDIA, Microsoft Azure, AWS, Google Cloud

Layer 2: Foundation Models

Pre-trained large models

Cost: $10M-$100M+ to train

Players: OpenAI, Anthropic, Google, Meta

Layer 3: Tools & Platforms

APIs, development tools, orchestration

Players:

LangChain

,

LlamaIndex

, Vector databases

Business opportunity area

Layer 4: Applications

Vertical-specific solutions

Where most businesses will compete

Examples: Legal AI, medical AI, marketing AI

Generative AI :

Value Chain and Stack

Where Should You Focus?

Most businesses: Layer 4 (Applications) using Layer 2 (APIs)

Don't build models from scratch unless you're Google/OpenAI

Focus on unique data, workflows, and domain expertise

## Slide 17

Large Language Models (LLM):

Overview

What is a Large Language Model (LLM)?

Definition:

A Large Language Model is a neural network trained on massive amounts of text data (trillions of words) to understand and generate human-like text. It predicts the probability of word sequences and generates coherent, contextually appropriate responses.

The "Large" in LLM:

Scale Matters:

Parameters:

Billions to trillions of adjustable values

Training data:

Hundreds of billions to trillions of words

Compute:

Thousands of GPUs, months of training

Cost:

$10 million to $100+ million per model

## Slide 20

Large Language Models (LLM):

Tokens

Not words:

Subword

units

Example:

"ChatGPT" = 2 tokens ("Chat" + "GPT")

"I'm" = 2 tokens ("I" + "'m")

Average:

~0.75 words per token in English

Why

subwords

:

Handle rare words, typos,

other languages efficiently

## Slide 21

Allianz

3211

Large Language Models (LLM):

Tokens

## Slide 22

What is Tokenization?

Tokenization

is the process of breaking down text into smaller units called

tokens

that language models can understand and process. Think of it as translating human language into a format the AI can work with.

Key Concept:

LLMs don't read text the way humans do. They convert text into numbers (tokens), process those numbers, and convert back to text.

Why Tokenization Matters

1. Cost Implications

Most LLM APIs charge by token, not by word

Understanding tokens helps you estimate and control costs

Example: "Hello" = 1 token, but "Hello!" might be 2 tokens

2. Context Window Limits

Models have token limits (e.g., 8K, 32K, 128K, 200K tokens)

Your prompt + output must fit within this limit

Exceeding the limit = truncation or errors

Large Language Models (LLM):

Tokenization

3. Performance Impact

More tokens = slower processing

Token efficiency = faster responses

Better prompts use tokens wisely

4. Understanding Model Behavior

Why the model struggles with certain words

Why it's better at English than other languages

Why it can't spell words backward reliably

## Slide 23

(Empty or no text content)

## Slide 24

From Data to Deployment - 4 Phases

Phase 1: Pre-training

⏱️ Months

What: Model learns from billions of web pages, books, code, etc.

Data: Trillions of tokens (1 trillion tokens ≈ 750 billion words)

Cost: $10M - $100M+ in compute

Goal: General language understanding

Example: Reading all of Wikipedia 10,000+ times

Phase 2: Fine-tuning

⏱️ Days to Weeks

What: Refine model for specific tasks or domains

Data: Thousands to millions of task-specific examples

Cost: $1,000 - $100,000

Goal: Specialized performance

Example: Medical diagnosis, legal analysis, coding

Large Language Models (LLM):

Training Process

## Slide 25

From Data to Deployment - 4 Phases

Phase 3: RLHF (Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback)

⏱️ Ongoing

What: Align model behavior with human preferences

Process: Humans rate different responses (helpful, harmless, honest)

Goal: Make model more useful and safe

Why it matters: Raw models can be offensive or unhelpful

Phase 4: Inference

⏱️ Real-time

What: Serving the model to end users

Cost per request: $0.001 - $0.10 depending on model

Speed: Seconds to minutes

Optimization: Caching, quantization, pruning

Critical Business Insight: Most companies will use pre-trained models via API, NOT build from scratch

Focus on: Prompt engineering, fine-tuning, and integration

Your competitive advantage: Data, workflows, domain expertise

Large Language Models (LLM):

Training Process

## Slide 26

(Empty or no text content)

## Slide 27

What Are Parameters?

Adjustable settings that control how AI models generate responses - like dials on a machine that affect output quality, consistency, and cost.

1. TEMPERATURE: The Creativity Dial

Scale: 0.0 to 1.0

Low Temperature (0.0 - 0.3): Predictable & Consistent

The model gives focused, deterministic answers. Same question = same answer every time.

Medium Temperature (0.5 - 0.7): Balanced - DEFAULT

Good mix of consistency and natural variation. Most common business setting.

High Temperature (0.8 - 1.0): Creative & Varied

Produces diverse, unexpected, creative outputs. More risk of inaccuracy.

Large Language Models (LLM):

Parameters

## Slide 28

2. CONTEXT WINDOW: The Memory Limit

How much text the AI can "remember" and process at once

Small Context (4K-8K tokens = ~3K-6K words)

Enough for simple Q&A and brief conversations.

Cost:

Low

Medium Context (16K-32K tokens = ~12K-24K words)

Can handle extended conversations and moderate documents.

Cost:

Medium

Large Context (128K-200K tokens = ~96K-150K words)

Can process entire books, large contracts, extensive codebases.

Cost:

High (2-3x more than small context)

Ultra Context (1M+ tokens = ~750K+ words)

Can process multiple documents, entire product documentation, massive codebases.

Cost:

Very high (5-10x more)

Key Decision: Match context size to actual need. Don't pay for 200K context when 8K works fine

.

Large Language Models (LLM):

Parameters

## Slide 29

3. MAX TOKENS: Response Length Control

Sets the maximum length of generated response

50-256 tokens (~40-200 words)

Short, direct answers.

Use for:

Classifications, yes/no questions, category labels, simple queries

512-1024 tokens (~400-750 words)

Standard response length.

Use for:

Chat responses, emails, summaries, explanations, basic analysis

2048-4096 tokens (~1,500-3,000 words)

Long-form content.

Use for:

Articles, detailed reports, comprehensive analyses, documentation, complex code

Important: Set max tokens slightly higher than expected need. If too low, responses get cut off mid-sentence. If unnecessarily high, you pay for unused capacity.

Large Language Models (LLM):

Parameters

## Slide 30

Bonus Parameters (Advanced)

Frequency Penalty (0.0 - 1.0)

Reduces word repetition based on how often words appear. Higher values = less repetition.

Use when:

Generating varied marketing content, avoiding robotic responses

Presence Penalty (0.0 - 1.0)

Encourages introducing new topics/words. Higher values = more diverse content.

Use when:

Brainstorming, exploring different angles

Top-P / Nucleus Sampling (0.0 - 1.0)

Alternative to temperature for controlling randomness.

Note: Use temperature OR top-p, not both. Most businesses should stick with temperature.

Large Language Models (LLM):

Parameters

## Slide 31

Real-World Configuration Examples

Customer Service Chatbot

Temperature:

0.3 (consistent, reliable answers)

Context Window:

16K (remember full conversation history)

Max Tokens:

800 (complete but concise responses)

Why:

Need accurate, consistent answers while maintaining conversation context

Legal Document Analysis

Temperature:

0.1 (maximum accuracy and precision)

Context Window:

128K+ (see entire contract)

Max Tokens:

2000 (detailed analysis)

Why:

Accuracy critical, need full document context, comprehensive output

Marketing Content Generation

Temperature:

0.8 (creative and varied)

Context Window:

8K (sufficient for brief context)

Max Tokens:

1000 (medium-length content)

Why:

Want creative variety, don't need much history, moderate length

## Slide 32

Data Analysis & Classification

Temperature:

0.0 (completely deterministic)

Context Window:

4K (just need the data)

Max Tokens:

100 (just the classification/answer)

Why:

Need identical results every time, minimal context needed

Code Generation

Temperature:

0.2 (mostly consistent but some flexibility)

Context Window:

32K (see related code files)

Max Tokens:

2000 (complete functions with comments)

Why:

Want reliable code patterns while seeing full context

Real-World Configuration Examples

## Slide 33

1. Temperature is your primary quality control

Low = Reliable and consistent (compliance, analysis, facts)

High = Creative and varied (marketing, ideation)

Wrong temperature = Either boring content OR unreliable answers

2. Context window affects both capability and cost

Larger contexts can process more information

But cost increases 2-10x for larger windows

Match to actual need, don't over-buy

3. One size does NOT fit all

Different applications need different settings

Configure per use case, not globally

Test and optimize based on real results

4. Small changes = Big impact

Adjusting temperature from 0.7 to 0.3 dramatically changes output

Wrong max tokens wastes money or cuts off responses

Parameter tuning can improve quality 30-50%

Large Language Models (LLM):

Parameters  Consideration

## Slide 34

Common Mistakes to Avoid

❌ Using high temperature for factual/compliance tasks → inconsistent answers

❌ Paying for massive context windows you don't need → 3-5x budget waste

❌ Setting max tokens too low → responses cut off mid-sentence

❌ Using same settings for all applications → suboptimal results everywhere

❌ Never testing or optimizing → missing 30-50% quality improvements

Large Language Models (LLM):

Parameters Consideration

## Slide 35

Large Language Models (LLM):

Evaluation

Key Evaluation Dimensions:

Accuracy:

Is the information factually correct?

Relevance:

Does it address the prompt effectively?

Coherence:

Is it logical and well-structured?

Fluency:

Is the language natural and grammatical?

Safety:

Is it free from harmful or biased content?

Evaluation Methods

Human Evaluation

Experts rate outputs on quality dimensions

✓ Most reliable | ✗ Expensive, slow, subjective

Automated Metrics

BLEU, ROUGE, perplexity, etc.

✓ Fast, scalable | ✗ May miss nuance

LLM-as-Judge

Use another LLM to evaluate outputs

✓ Scalable, nuanced | ✗ Requires careful prompt design

Common Challenges

Hallucinations

Models can confidently generate false information

Bias

Trained on internet data, may reflect societal biases

Context Limits

Can only process limited text at once (though improving)

Inconsistency

Same prompt may yield different outputs

Knowledge Cutoff

Training data has a cutoff date

## Slide 51

Large Language Models (LLM):

Open Source Vs Enterprise LLMs

Open Source LLMs

(e.g., Llama, Mistral, Falcon)

Advantages:

Cost flexibility

- No licensing fees, pay only for infrastructure

Customization

- Full control to fine-tune models for specific use cases

Transparency

- Can inspect model architecture and understand how it works

No vendor lock-in

- Freedom to switch or modify without contractual constraints

Data privacy

- Can run entirely on-premises, keeping sensitive data internal

Challenges:

Infrastructure burden

- Need technical expertise and hardware to deploy

Support responsibility

- No guaranteed vendor support or SLAs

Maintenance overhead

- Updates, security patches, and optimization are your responsibility

Hidden costs

- Engineering time, cloud compute, and ongoing management can add up

## Slide 52

Large Language Models (LLM):

Open Source Vs Enterprise LLMs

Enterprise LLMs

(e.g., Claude, GPT-4, Gemini)

Advantages:

Ease of deployment

- API-based, ready to use immediately

Performance

- Often more capable models with better results

Vendor support

- SLAs, technical assistance, and regular updates

Security & compliance

- Enterprise-grade security certifications

and compliance frameworks

Scalability

- Automatic scaling without infrastructure management

Challenges:

Ongoing costs

- Per-token pricing can become expensive at scale

Limited customization

- Less flexibility to modify core models

Vendor dependency

- Reliance on provider's roadmap and pricing

Data considerations

- Need to trust vendor with data (though many offer privacy guarantees)

## Slide 53

The Process (Simplified):

Step 1:

Start with random noise (pure static)

Step 2:

Gradually remove noise, guided by:

Text prompt (e.g., "a sunset over mountains")

Training on millions of image-caption pairs

Understanding: What do mountains look like? What is sunset lighting?

Step 3:

After 20-50 denoising steps, coherent image emerges

Analogy:

Like a sculptor chipping away marble, guided by a vision - but working from chaos to order

## Slide 59

⚠️

Copyright & Legal Issues

Training data includes copyrighted images

Ongoing lawsuits (Getty Images vs. Stability AI)

Output ownership is unclear in many jurisdictions

Use commercially-safe options (Firefly) for business

⚠️

Consistency Challenges

Hard to generate same character/product across multiple images

Solutions: Reference images, fine-tuning, specialized tools

⚠️

Text in Images

Most models struggle with readable text

Letters often garbled or nonsensical

Improving but still unreliable

⚠️

Bias & Stereotypes

Can perpetuate stereotypes from training data

Example: "CEO" often generates white male images

Requires careful prompt engineering and review

Image Generation :

Important Considerations

## Slide 60

Two Main Approaches:

Approach 1: Frame-by-Frame Generation

Generate individual frames as images

Use interpolation to create smooth transitions

Pros: Can leverage existing image models

Cons: Inconsistency across frames, artifacts

Approach 2: Diffusion in Space + Time

Generate video directly with temporal consistency

Model learns physics, motion, object permanence

Pros: More coherent, realistic motion

Cons: Computationally intensive, newer technology

## Slide 64

⚠️ Physics Inconsistencies

Objects sometimes behave unrealistically

Gravity, collisions can be wrong

Example: Water flowing upward, impossible movements

⚠️ Length Limitations

Most models: 3-10 seconds

Sora: Up to 60 seconds (rare)

Longer videos require stitching clips

⚠️ Cost & Compute

More expensive than image generation

Rendering can take minutes to hours

High-quality = higher cost

Video Generation :

Current Limitations

⚠️ Control & Precision

Difficult to specify exact camera movements

Hard to maintain character consistency

Limited editing capabilities after generation

⚠️ Uncanny Valley for Humans

Human faces/movements still often look "off"

Better for abstract, stylized, or non-human content

Bottom Line: Video AI is impressive but still early stage. Best for supplementing, not replacing, traditional video production.

Weak Prompt

Analyze Airbnb’s strategy.

Strong Prompt

Present analysis in structured bullet points.

Conclude with 3 strategic risks and 2 investment implications.

## Slide 79

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

Zero-Shot Prompting

What is Zero-Shot?

Asking AI to perform a task

without providing any examples

- just direct instructions.

When to Use

✅

Good for:

Simple tasks, common operations, quick queries

❌

Not ideal for:

Complex tasks, domain-specific work, when consistency is critical

Trade-off:

Fast and simple, but less reliable for complex work

## Slide 80

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

Few-Shot Prompting

What is Few-Shot?

Provide

2-5 examples

of the task, then AI follows the pattern.

Principle:

Show, don't just tell.

When to Use

✅

Good for:

Specific formats, brand voice, custom logic, consistency

Impact:

Much more accurate than zero-shot. 3-5 examples is optimal.

## Slide 81

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

Chain of Thought

CoT

Prompting

What is Chain-of-Thought?

Asking AI to

show its reasoning step-by-step

before the final answer.

Key phrase:

"Think step-by-step" or "Show your work“

## Slide 82

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

Chain of Thought

CoT

Prompting

When to Use

✅

Essential for:

Complex calculations

Multi-step reasoning

Business analysis

Financial modeling

Troubleshooting

Impact:

Can improve accuracy from 60% → 95% on complex problems

## Slide 83

Self Consistency: Chain of Thought + Democracy

## Slide 84

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

Self Consistency Prompting

What is Self-Consistency?

Generate

multiple responses

to the same prompt, then choose the most common or best answer.

Principle:

Wisdom of crowds - if AI gives same answer 8 out of 10 times, it's likely correct.

## Slide 85

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

Self Consistency Prompting

When to Use

✅

Critical for:

High-stakes decisions

When accuracy is paramount

Ambiguous or complex questions

Verification of important outputs

Implementation:

Run same prompt 3-5 times

Compare results

Choose consensus answer or aggregate insights

Trade-off:

Higher accuracy but 3-5x cost and time

## Slide 86

Tree of Thought : Chain of Thought + Gaming

=  One Winner

## Slide 87

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

Tree of Thoughts Prompting

What is Tree-of-Thoughts?

AI explores

multiple reasoning paths simultaneously

, evaluates each, then chooses the best solution.

Analogy:

Like brainstorming 3-4 different approaches before deciding which is best.

## Slide 88

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

Tree of Thoughts Prompting

When to Use

✅

Best for:

Strategic decisions

Complex problem-solving

When multiple solutions exist

Trade-off analysis

Trade-off:

More thorough but slower and more expensive (multiple reasoning paths)

## Slide 89

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

ReAct

(Reasoning + Acting) Prompting

What is

ReAct

?

AI alternates between

reasoning

about what to do and

acting

(using tools/taking steps), then observes results and adjusts.

Key concept:

Think → Act → Observe → Think → Act (iterative loop)

## Slide 90

Prompt Engineering Techniques:

ReAct

(Reasoning + Acting) Prompting

When to Use

✅

Essential for:

Tasks requiring multiple tools or data sources

Research and information gathering

Complex workflows

When AI needs to verify information

Multi-step processes

This is how AI agents work

-

ReAct

enables autonomous task completion

## Slide 102

LLMs Have a Knowledge Limit

The Challenge:

Training data cuts off (e.g., January 2025)

No access to YOUR proprietary data

Can't retrieve real-time information

No knowledge of internal documents

Makes up information when uncertain (hallucination)

Real Cost:

Company loses $2M deal because AI gave outdated product info

Traditional Solutions (Pre-RAG)

Option 1: Fine-tuning

❌ Expensive ($10K-100K+)

❌ Time-consuming (weeks)

❌ Static (data becomes outdated)

❌ Requires ML expertise

Retreival Augmented Generation RAG :

The Fundamental Problem

Option 2: Prompting with full context

❌ Limited by context window (200K tokens = ~150K words)

❌ Expensive (higher token costs)

❌ Slow processing

❌ Not scalable

Implement re-ranking

Response Validation

Implement confidence scoring

Human review for critical applications

## Page 9

Agentic AI : 
Be Careful between AI agents and Classic AI Bots
An AI agent possesses four core properties:
1.Perception — reads context (documents, APIs, UI, sensors)
2.Reasoning — plans multi-step actions toward a goal
3.Action — calls tools, writes code, sends emails, queries DBs
4.Memory — retains context across steps (short-term) and sessions (long-term)
The Agentic Spectrum

(No extractable text)

## Page 17

Agentic AI:
What Enabled This Transitions ?
1. Function Calling / Tool Use 
What it means: AI can now use software tools
Before: AI could only generate text
After: AI can:
•Make API calls
•Query databases
•Send emails
•Update CRM systems
•Execute code
•Search the web
Example:
•Old: "Here's an email draft" (you send it)
•New: AI drafts AND sends the email itself

## Page 18

Agentic AI:
What Enabled This Transition?
2. Extended Context Windows
What it means: AI can remember much more
Evolution:
•2022: 4K tokens (~3K words)
•2023: 32K tokens (~24K words)
•2024-2025: 200K-2M tokens (~150K-1.5M words)
Impact: Can handle entire documents, long conversations, complex workflows without forgetting
Example: Analyze 50-page contract + company policies + past precedents in one go

## Page 19

3. Improved Reasoning (Chain-of-Thought)
What it means: AI can think through complex problems
Breakthrough: Models can now:
•Break down complex tasks
•Plan multi-step solutions
•Evaluate options
•Adjust based on results
Example:
•Before: "I can't book that meeting" (gives up)
•After: "Time conflict detected. Checking alternatives. Proposing 3 other slots. Would you prefer morning 
or afternoon?"
Agentic AI:
What Enabled This Transition?

## Page 20

4. Multi-Modal Capabilities
What it means: AI understands images, audio, video + text
Impact: Can now:
•Analyze documents with images
•Understand screenshots
•Process diagrams and charts
•Watch videos and summarize
•Listen to calls and provide insights
Example: Show AI a screenshot of an error → It diagnoses and fixes the issue
Agentic AI:
What Enabled This Transition?

## Page 21

5. Reduced Costs (10-100x Cheaper)
Price Evolution:
•2022: GPT-3 → $0.02 per 1K tokens
•2024: GPT-4 class → $0.01 per 1K tokens
•2024: Efficient models → $0.001 per 1K tokens
Impact: Makes large-scale deployment economically viable
Example: Running 1M customer service conversations:
•2022 cost: $20,000
•2024 cost: $500-2,000
Agentic AI:
What Enabled This Transition?

## Page 22

Agentic AI:
Anatomy of AI Agents

## Page 23

Agentic AI:
Anatomy of AI Agents
Key Reasoning Patterns
•ReAct (Reason + Act): Think → Act → Observe → Repeat
•Chain-of-Thought (CoT): Break complex reasoning into steps
•Tree of Thoughts: Explore multiple solution paths simultaneously
•Plan-and-Execute: Separate planning from execution for long-horizon tasks
Memory Architecture
Type Analogy Business Use Case
In-context (working) RAM Current conversation, task state
External (vector DB) Hard Drive Company knowledge base, past 
interactions
Episodic Personal diary Agent learning from past jobs
Procedural Skills How to handle refund requests

## Page 24

Agentic AI:
Anatomy of AI Agents
Multi-Agents Systems
•Orchestrator Agent: Delegates tasks to specialized sub-agents 
•Worker Agents: Execute specific functions (research, coding, communication) 
•Critic/Validator Agent: Reviews outputs for quality and compliance 
•Communication protocols: MCP (Model Context Protocol), A2A (Agent-to-Agent)

## Page 35

Agentic AI:
Landmark Real-World Deployments (2025-2026)
•Salesforce Agentforce: Autonomous sales and service agents embedded in CRM

•ServiceNow AI Agents: IT and HR workflow automation 
•Klarna AI Agent: Handles customer service at the scale of 700 human FTEs 
•Cognition Devin: Fully autonomous software engineering agent 
•Harvey AI: Legal research and document drafting for law firms 
•Cohere Command A: Agentic enterprise RAG at scale

## Page 36

Agentic AI:
The Trust Problem – What Can Go Wrong ?
• Hallucination with action: Agent acts on false information
• Prompt injection: Malicious content hijacks agent behavior
• Scope creep: Agent takes unintended actions (e.g., deletes files)
• Runaway loops: Agent stuck in infinite retry cycles
• Data leakage: Agent exposes sensitive information through tool calls
The "Human in the Loop" Spectrum

## Page 37

Agentic AI:
Governance and Regulatory 
Governance Framework for Agentic AI
1.Define agent scope: What can it access? What actions are permitted?
2.Establish audit trails: Every agent action must be logged
3.Set kill switches: Mechanisms to halt agents instantly
4.Implement sandboxing: Test before production deployment
5.Define accountability: Who is responsible when an agent fails?
6.Monitor continuously: Behavioral drift detection
 Regulatory Landscape
•EU AI Act (2024): Risk-tiered regulation — autonomous agents in high-risk domains face 
strict requirements
•GDPR implications: Agent memory and data retention
•SEC guidance: AI use in financial advice and trading
•Emerging: Agent identity standards, liability frameworks

## Page 38

Agentic AI:
What’s next ? 
Near-Term Horizon (2026–2027)
•Multimodal agents: Seeing, hearing, and acting on video/images/voice
•Computer-use agents: AI controlling desktop UIs and browsers autonomously
•Persistent agents: Always-on agents with continuous memory across months
•Agent marketplaces: Buy/deploy pre-built specialized agents like SaaS apps
•Vertical agent platforms: Industry-specific (legal, finance, healthcare) agent stacks
Medium-Term Horizon (2027–2030)
•Agent-to-Agent economies: Agents hiring and paying other agents
•Self-improving agents: Agents that fine-tune themselves from experience
•Physical AI: Agents controlling robots, drones, manufacturing systems
•Collective intelligence: Thousands of agents collaborating on complex problems

## Page 39

Agentic RAG:
Overview
Agentic RAG gives the retrieval process a brain. Instead of one fixed retrieve → generate step, an agent 
decides how, when, where, and how many times to retrieve — and can act on what it finds.

## Page 40

Agentic RAG :
Core Capabilities 
Capability What it means Business Impact
Query decomposition Breaks complex questions into 
sub-queries
Handles nuanced, multi-part 
business questions
Adaptive retrieval Decides whether to retrieve at all Faster responses, lower cost
Multi-source retrieval Searches across databases, web, 
APIs simultaneously Richer, more accurate answers
Iterative refinement Re-queries if initial results are 
insufficient Higher answer quality
Self-evaluation Grades its own retrieval before 
generating Reduces hallucination
Tool-augmented retrieval Runs code or queries DBs as part 
of retrieval
Handles structured + 
unstructured data

## Page 41

Agentic RAG :
Different Architectures 
1. Corrective RAG (CRAG) Agent evaluates retrieved documents → if low confidence, triggers a web search 
fallback.
Use case: Compliance agents that need up-to-date regulatory information.
2. Self-RAG Agent decides at every step: "Do I need to retrieve? Is this retrieved doc relevant? Is my answer 
supported?"
Use case: Medical or legal Q&A where grounding is critical.
3. Adaptive RAG Routes queries dynamically: simple questions → direct LLM answer; complex ones → full 
retrieval pipeline.
Use case: Customer service bots handling both FAQs and complex account issues.
4. Multi-Agent RAG Specialized agents retrieve from different sources (internal DB, web, CRM) and an 
orchestrator synthesizes results.
Use case: Investment research combining filings, news, and internal analyst notes.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

2 / 48

01

Foundations of Machine Learning

What is ML, why it matters for business, and how it fits into the broader AI ecosystem.

AI vs ML vs DS

Business Value

Types of Learning

Foundations

What is Machine Learning?

Teaching computers to learn from data — not explicit rules

📖 Arthur Samuel (1959)

"The field of study that gives computers the ability to learn without being explicitly programmed."

Traditional Programming vs ML

Traditional:

Data + Rules → Answers

Machine Learning:

Data + Answers → Rules (Model)

ML finds

patterns

in data automatically

Improves with more data and feedback

Generalizes to

unseen examples

Replaces manual rule-writing with statistical inference

Business Analogy

🏦

Loan Approval:

Old: A manager writes 200 explicit rules. ML way: Feed 100,000 historical decisions. The model discovers the patterns itself — and often outperforms the rulebook.

Three Core Ingredients

📊

Data

The fuel

⚙️

Algorithm

The engine

🎯

Model

The output

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

4 / 48

Foundations

AI vs ML vs Data Science — Untangling the Buzzwords

🤖 Artificial Intelligence

The broad discipline of building systems that exhibit intelligent behaviour. Includes rule-based systems, expert systems, robotics, ML, and more.

Broadest umbrella

🧠 Machine Learning

A subset of AI. Systems that learn statistical patterns from data. Includes classical ML, deep learning, reinforcement learning.

Subset of AI

📊 Data Science

The ML Project Lifecycle

From business problem to production value

🎯

1. Problem

Business goal, KPIs

📥

2. Data Collection

Sources, volume

🧹

3. Data Prep

Cleaning, EDA

⚙️

Train, tune, eval

🚀

5. Deployment

API, monitoring

📈

6. Impact

ROI, feedback

📌 80/20 Reality:

Data scientists spend ~80% of time on data collection and preparation — only ~20% on actual modelling. The quality of your data is the biggest lever on model performance.

Common Pitfall

Jumping straight to algorithms without clearly defining the business problem. Many expensive ML projects fail because the question was wrong, not the model.

Best Practice

Always start with: "What decision will this model support, and what does a wrong prediction cost?" This defines your success metric before writing a line of code.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

8 / 48

Foundations

Data Quality — The Silent Killer of ML Projects

"Garbage in, garbage out"

The Six Dimensions of Data Quality

Completeness

— Are there missing values? Why are they missing?

Accuracy

— Does the data correctly represent reality?

Consistency

— Same entity recorded the same way across systems?

Timeliness

— Is the data fresh enough for the decision?

Relevance

— Does this feature actually relate to the outcome?

Volume

— Enough samples for the model to generalise?

⚠️ Label Leakage

Including data in training that wouldn't be available at prediction time. 99% accuracy — completely useless in production.

🔍 Class Imbalance

In fraud detection, <0.1% of transactions are fraud. A model predicting "not fraud" achieves 99.9% accuracy — yet catches zero fraudsters.

✅ Rule of Thumb

More and better data consistently beats more complex algorithms. A simple model on clean data outperforms a complex model on dirty data.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

11 / 48

Supervised · Classification

Logistic Regression

Simple, fast, explainable — the baseline every project should start with

Extends linear regression to output a

probability (0–1)

via the sigmoid function

Draws a

linear decision boundary

between classes

Coefficients show feature importance — highly interpretable

Output: "P(churn) = 0.73" — a confidence score, not just a label

✅ When to Use

Binary classification, need for interpretability (regulated industries), fast baseline

❌ Limitations

Assumes linear relationship. Struggles with complex, non-linear patterns.

Business Case: Churn Prediction

Telecom trains on 6 months of data.

Key coefficients:

+0.8 — Support calls in 30 days

+0.5 — Month-to-month contract

–0.6 — Tenure > 24 months

Marketing knows exactly which levers to pull.

Sigmoid Curve

0

1

0.5 threshold

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

12 / 48

Supervised · Classification

k-Nearest Neighbors (kNN)

"Tell me who your neighbours are, and I'll tell you who you are"

No training phase — stores all data points

At prediction: find the

k most similar examples

Majority vote determines the class

Similarity by distance (Euclidean, Manhattan…)

Small k = complex boundary, large k = smooth boundary

📌 The k Trade-off

k=1: flexible, risk of overfitting. k=large: smooth, risk of underfitting. Cross-validation finds the sweet spot.

✅ Strengths

Simple, no training, multi-class, good for recommendations

❌ Weaknesses

Slow at prediction, sensitive to irrelevant features

? query

k=3: 3 blue → Class A wins

1 red inside radius → outvoted

Supervised · Classification

Decision Trees

The most interpretable model — like a flowchart that learned from data

Income > €40,000?

No

Missed Payments > 2?

Yes

🔴 Default

No

🟡 Monitor

Yes

Credit Score > 650?

Yes

🟢 Approve

No

🟡 Review

Splits data using

impurity measures

(Gini, Entropy)

Extremely interpretable — show to a bank regulator

Handles categorical & numerical features natively

Prone to

overfitting

if not pruned (set max_depth)

Regulatory Advantage

GDPR & Basel III require explainability. Decision Trees let you print the reason for any prediction.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

14 / 48

Supervised · Classification

Random Forest

The wisdom of crowds — many imperfect trees beat one perfect tree

How It Works

Original Dataset (N rows, M features)

↓ Bootstrap Sampling (with replacement)

Tree 1

Random subset

Tree 2

Different subset

Tree N…

↓ Majority Vote

🏆 Final Prediction

Reduces variance through

ensemble averaging

Feature importance scores — great for business interpretation

Robust to outliers and missing data

Works well out-of-the-box with minimal tuning

📌 Business Case: Fraud Detection

A European fintech processes 1M transactions/day, flagging 2% for review — catching 85% of actual fraud with <0.1% false positive rate.

✅ Best For

Tabular data, feature selection, production baselines

⚙️ Key Params

n_estimators, max_depth, max_features

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

15 / 48

Supervised · Classification

Support Vector Machines (SVM)

Find the widest street between classes — maximum margin classification

Hyperplane

Support Vectors

← Margin →

Finds the

hyperplane that maximizes margin

between classes

Kernel trick

: maps data to higher dimensions for non-linear problems

Only support vectors define the model — memory efficient

Excellent for high-dimensional, small-to-medium datasets

Business Use: Text Classification

Before deep learning dominated NLP, SVMs were gold standard for sentiment analysis and spam detection — still competitive for smaller datasets.

✅ Strengths

High accuracy, works in high dimensions, robust to overfitting

❌ Weaknesses

Slow on large datasets, kernel selection tricky

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

16 / 48

Supervised · Classification

Naïve Bayes & Gradient Boosting (XGBoost)

Naïve Bayes

Based on Bayes' Theorem: updates beliefs with evidence

"Naïve" = assumes features are

conditionally independent

Extremely fast, works with tiny datasets

Excels in

text classification

(spam, sentiment)

📧 "Buy Now Cheap Free" — Naïve Bayes multiplies P(each word|spam) to get P(spam). Simple math, powerful result.

Gradient Boosting / XGBoost

Builds trees

sequentially

— each corrects previous errors

Optimises a loss function using gradient descent

Dominates

Kaggle competitions

on tabular data

Best-in-class for fraud, churn, credit risk at scale

🏆 Industry Standard

XGBoost / LightGBM / CatBoost are the go-to models in banking, insurance, and e-commerce. If you learn one advanced classifier — learn gradient boosting.

Banking

Insurance

E-commerce

Healthcare

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

18 / 48

Supervised · Evaluation

Model Evaluation: The Confusion Matrix

Accuracy alone is misleading — here's why

Confusion Matrix (Fraud Detection)

Predicted →

Pred: Fraud

Pred: Legit

Actual: Fraud

TP 850

FN 150

Actual: Legit

FP 200

TN 98,800

🟢 TP: Caught fraud · 🔴 FP: False alarm · 🔴 FN: Missed · 🟢 TN: Cleared

Key Metrics

Accuracy

= (TP+TN)/All = 99.6% ← misleading!

Precision

= TP/(TP+FP) = 81% ← of flagged, how many are real fraud?

Recall

= TP/(TP+FN) = 85% ← of all fraud, how many caught?

F1 Score

= 2×(P×R)/(P+R) = 83% ← balances both

⚖️ Business Trade-off

High Recall = fewer missed frauds. High Precision = fewer false alarms. Business goals determine the balance — not the data scientist alone.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

19 / 48

Supervised · Model Quality

Overfitting vs Underfitting — The Bias-Variance Trade-off

📉

Underfitting

Too simple. High bias. Poor on train AND test.

🎯

Just Right

Generalises well. Good on train AND test.

📈

Overfitting

Memorised data. Great train, poor test.

Model Complexity →

Train

Test

Sweet Spot

Problem

Solutions

Underfitting

Add features, more complex model, remove regularisation

Overfitting

More data, regularisation (L1/L2), pruning, cross-validation

🔄 Cross-Validation

K-Fold CV: split into K parts, train on K-1, test on 1. Repeat K times. Average = reliable estimate. Essential for any production model.

Business Reality:

A model perfect on training data but poor in production has cost companies millions in bad decisions.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

20 / 48

03

Supervised Learning:

Regression

Predicting continuous values — revenue, prices, demand, KPIs.

Linear Regression

Ridge & Lasso

Ensembles

Demand Forecasting

Supervised · Regression

Linear Regression

The foundation of predictive analytics — fit a line through data

Predicts a

continuous numeric value

(price, revenue, score)

Fits: ŷ = β₀ + β₁x₁ + β₂x₂ + ... + βₙxₙ

Minimises

Mean Squared Error

(sum of squared residuals)

Each coefficient = impact of that feature on the outcome

"Each extra bedroom adds €12,000 to house price"

📌 Assumptions

Linearity, homoscedasticity, no multicollinearity, normally distributed residuals.

Evaluation Metrics

MAE, RMSE, R² (variance explained). R²=0.85 means "85% of price variation explained by features."

Case: Housing Price Prediction

Size (m²)

ŷ = 1200x + 85,000

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

22 / 48

Supervised · Regression

Ridge, Lasso & Polynomial Regression

Preventing overfitting and capturing non-linear patterns

Ridge Regression (L2)

Penalty = λ × Σ(β²). Shrinks all coefficients toward zero but never exactly zero. Good for multicollinearity.

Lasso Regression (L1)

Penalty = λ × Σ|β|. Can shrink to

exactly zero

— automatic feature selection! 50 features → Lasso keeps the 8 most important.

Elastic Net

Combines L1 and L2. Best of both worlds for high-dimensional data.

Polynomial Regression

Adds polynomial features: x², x³ to capture curves

Still "linear" in terms of parameters — same math, richer features

⚠️ Degree too high → extreme overfitting

Linear (poor)

Polynomial degree=2 ✓

Regression in Business — Applied Examples

🏠

Housing Price Prediction

Zillow, ImmobilienScout24 use gradient boosting. Features: location, size, floor, age. Output: estimated price ± confidence interval.

R² ≈ 0.90

📦

Demand Forecasting

Amazon, REWE, DHL predict units per SKU. Features: history, promotions, weather, holidays. Reduces overstock & stockouts by 20-35%.

Cost Reduction

📊

KPI & Revenue Prediction

Marketing mix models regress revenue on ad spend. Output: attribution per channel and ROI-maximising budget allocation.

Strategic Planning

Algorithm

Best For

Key Advantage

Watch Out For

Linear Regression

Baseline, interpretable use cases

Coefficients = direct insight

Assumes linearity

Ridge / Lasso

Many features, risk of overfitting

Built-in feature selection (Lasso)

Hyperparameter tuning needed

Regression Trees

Non-linear, interactions

No distributional assumptions

Overfits without pruning

Gradient Boosting

Complex patterns, production

State-of-the-art accuracy

Less interpretable, slower

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

26 / 48

Unsupervised · Clustering

K-Means Clustering

Partition data into K groups by minimizing within-cluster variance

Algorithm Steps

1

Initialise K centroids randomly

2

Assign each point to nearest centroid

(Euclidean distance)

3

Recompute centroids as cluster mean

4

Repeat until convergence

Choosing K: The Elbow Method

Plot inertia vs K. Look for the "elbow" where adding more clusters yields diminishing returns. Typically K=3–7 for business segments.

📌 Business Case: Customer Segmentation

A retail bank clusters 500,000 customers on balance, transaction frequency, product holdings, tenure.

Result — 4 segments:

🔵 "High-Value Savers" → Priority service

🟢 "Young Transactors" → Digital products

🟡 "Dormant Holders" → Re-engagement

🔴 "Credit-Dependent" → Risk management

✅ Strengths

Simple, scalable, fast, interpretable

❌ Limitations

Must specify K, sensitive to outliers

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

27 / 48

Unsupervised · Clustering

Hierarchical Clustering & DBSCAN

When K-Means isn't enough

Hierarchical Clustering

Builds a tree of clusters (

dendrogram

)

Agglomerative: bottom-up, merge closest clusters iteratively

No need to pre-specify K — cut the tree at any level

Computationally expensive for large datasets

Business Use

Market hierarchy analysis — grouping products, brands, or regions into a logical tree. Used in pharma for patient profiling.

DBSCAN — Density-Based Clustering

Finds clusters of

arbitrary shape

— not just spheres

Labels sparse regions as

noise/outliers

automatically

Parameters: epsilon (radius), min_samples

No need to specify number of clusters

🎯 Killer Feature: Anomaly Detection

Points not belonging to any dense cluster = anomalies. Used in cybersecurity, manufacturing defect detection, and financial fraud.

Arbitrary Shapes

Outlier Detection

No K needed

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

28 / 48

Unsupervised · Dimensionality Reduction

Principal Component Analysis (PCA)

Compress information — keep what matters, discard what doesn't

With 100 features, distances become meaningless (

curse of dimensionality

)

PCA finds directions of

maximum variance

in high-dimensional space

Projects data onto fewer dimensions (Principal Components)

PC1 explains most variance, PC2 explains the next most, etc.

Business Example

A retailer has 200 product attributes. PCA reduces to 5 components explaining 92% of variance → model trains 10× faster, generalises better.

Use for

visualisation

(reduce to 2D to plot clusters)

Trade-off: interpretability lost (components = linear combinations)

Feature 1

PC1

PC2

2D data → compressed to PC1 (1D)

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

29 / 48

Unsupervised · Association Rules

Market Basket Analysis

"Customers who bought X also bought Y"

Key Concepts

Support

= How often {A, B} appear together / all transactions

Confidence

= P(B | A) — if customer buys A, how often do they buy B?

Lift

= Confidence / P(B) — is the association stronger than chance?

Lift > 1 = positive association | Algorithms:

Apriori, FP-Growth

Classic Example

🍺🍼 "Beer & Diapers" — Walmart found Friday evening beer and diaper purchases were highly correlated. Placing them near each other increased both.

Business Applications

🛒 E-commerce

Amazon "Frequently Bought Together". Up to 35% of Amazon's revenue comes from recommendations — largely association-rule based.

💳 Banking

Customers with mortgage AND checking account → high likelihood for savings account. Increase product holding per customer.

🏥 Healthcare

Drug interaction analysis — which medications frequently co-prescribed? Flag dangerous combinations automatically.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

30 / 48

05

Semi-Supervised Learning

When you have lots of data but labelling it all is too expensive or slow.

Label Propagation

Self-Training

Real-World Scarcity

Semi-Supervised Learning

The pragmatic middle ground — few labels, lots of unlabelled data

💰 The Labelling Problem

Labelling 1 medical scan: €50–200 (expert radiologist).

10,000 scans: up to €2,000,000.

But you have 100,000 unlabelled scans in archives.

Semi-supervised learning uses all of them.

How It Works

Self-Training

: Train on labelled → predict unlabelled → add high-confidence predictions → retrain

Label Propagation

: Spread labels through a graph of similar data points

Co-Training

: Two models teach each other from different feature views

🏥 Medical Imaging

Label 500 X-rays. Use semi-supervised on 50,000 unlabelled images. Performance approaches fully-supervised at 5% the labelling cost.

📝 Content Moderation

Social platforms receive millions of posts/day. Human moderators label thousands. Semi-supervised extends coverage across millions automatically.

🌍 Multi-Language NLP

Rich English labels + sparse German labels + large unlabelled German corpus → good German performance without full re-annotation.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

Reinforcement Learning

Learning through interaction — reward, trial, error, and optimisation.

Agent & Environment

Reward Signal

Pricing Optimisation

Reinforcement Learning

Reinforcement Learning — Learning by Doing

An agent learns to maximise cumulative reward through trial and error

Core Framework

🤖

Agent

Decision maker

⇄

🌍

Environment

The world it acts in

State

Current situation

Action

What agent does

Reward

Feedback signal

🎮 Like training a dog — tries actions, receives rewards/penalties, gradually learns the optimal policy.

Business Applications

💰

Dynamic Pricing

— Uber, airlines adjust price based on demand state

🛍️

Recommendations

— Netflix/Spotify maximise engagement (reward = watch time)

📈

Algorithmic Trading

— Agent decides buy/sell/hold to maximise return

🏭

Supply Chain

— Controls inventory reorder policies to minimise costs

⚠️ Complexity Warning

RL requires careful reward design, significant compute, and simulation environments. Start with supervised approaches if possible.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

34 / 48

07

Transfer Learning

Reusing knowledge learned in one domain to solve problems in another.

Pre-trained Models

Fine-Tuning

Domain Adaptation

Transfer Learning

Transfer Learning — Don't Start from Scratch

🧠 A radiologist trained on chest X-rays adapts quickly to knee MRIs. They don't start from scratch — they transfer learned patterns.

Similarly, GPT trained on 100B words can be fine-tuned on 1,000 legal contracts to become an expert legal analyser.

Process

1. Start with large pre-trained model (GPT, BERT, ResNet…)

2. Freeze early layers (generic patterns already learned)

3. Fine-tune later layers on your domain data

4. Evaluate on your specific business task

Business Use Cases

📄

Document Classification

— fine-tune BERT on insurance claims

😊

Sentiment Analysis

— adapt NLP model to financial news tone

🏭

Quality Control

— fine-tune image classifier on factory defects

🤖

Customer Service Bots

— fine-tune LLM on company FAQs

💊

Drug Discovery

— transfer chemical structure models to new molecules

💡 Business Impact:

500–5,000 domain-specific examples + a pre-trained base = production-ready model. No Google-scale compute required.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

36 / 48

Practical ML

Common Pitfalls in ML Projects

Why ML projects fail — and how to avoid it

❌ Wrong Problem Framing

Building an ML model when a SQL query would suffice. Always clarify: "What decision changes based on this output?"

❌ Data Leakage

Training on features that include future information. Results look spectacular — then the model fails in production.

❌ Ignoring Class Imbalance

99% accuracy on fraud detection sounds great until you realise the model says "not fraud" on everything.

⚠️ Overfitting to Val Set

Tuning 50 hyperparameters on your validation set makes it effectively another training set. Keep a final test set untouched.

⚠️ Feature Engineering Neglect

Jumping to complex models instead of creating better features. A smart feature on a simple model often wins.

⚠️ Deployment Afterthought

A model that can't integrate into the business system is worth nothing. Plan for inference latency and monitoring from day one.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

37 / 48

Responsible ML

Bias & Fairness in Machine Learning

Models learn from historical data — including its injustices

Sources of Bias

Historical Bias

— If past loan decisions discriminated by gender/race, a model trained on those decisions will replicate the discrimination

Sampling Bias

— Training data doesn't represent the full population

Label Bias

— Human annotators introduce their own biases into labels

Measurement Bias

— Features measured differently for different subgroups

⚖️ COMPAS Recidivism Tool

Used by US courts. Found to be twice as likely to falsely flag Black defendants as high risk vs white defendants. Legal and ethical crisis.

💳 Amazon Hiring Algorithm

Trained on historical data (mostly male). Began downgrading CVs mentioning "women's" clubs. Shelved after discovery.

✅ EU AI Act

High-risk AI systems (credit, hiring, healthcare) require bias audits and transparency. Non-compliance: up to €30M or 6% of global revenue.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

38 / 48

Business Strategy

ROI of Machine Learning Projects

How to justify investment and measure success

ROI Framework

Quantify the problem

: "We lose €2M/year to undetected fraud"

Estimate improvement

: "Model catches 40% more → saves €800K/year"

Calculate costs

: Data prep + development + infrastructure + maintenance

Net ROI

= (Savings − Costs) / Costs

Include

non-financial benefits

: speed, satisfaction, compliance

⏱️ Time to Value

Average enterprise ML project: 6–18 months to production. Prioritise quick wins to build internal credibility.

Real ROI Examples

Use Case

Investment

Annual Return

Churn Prediction

€800K+

Demand Forecasting

€200K

€1.2M

Fraud Detection

€300K

€3M+

Predictive Maintenance

€400K

€2.5M

€250K

€1.8M

💡 Key:

The best ML investments let humans focus on higher-value work while machines handle high-volume, repetitive decisions.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

39 / 48

Responsible ML

Model Explainability — Opening the Black Box

Why Explainability Matters

🏦

Regulation

— GDPR Article 22: right to explanation for automated decisions

🛡️

Trust

— Users won't adopt systems they don't understand

🔍

Debugging

— Understanding why a model fails helps fix it

⚖️

Fairness Auditing

— Reveals if protected attributes drive decisions

SHAP Values

Assigns each feature a contribution value per prediction. "Loan denied: low income −0.3, no credit history −0.4, high debt ratio −0.2."

LIME

Approximates any model locally with a simple interpretable model. Works for any algorithm — great for one-off explanations.

Feature Importance (Tree-based)

Random Forest and XGBoost natively provide importance scores — which variables drive model decisions most.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

40 / 48

Beyond Classical ML

A Brief Look at Deep Learning, NLP & Computer Vision

When classical ML isn't enough — and when it still is

🧠

Deep Learning

Multi-layer neural networks. Excels at images, audio, video, text. Requires large data and GPU compute. Not always the best choice.

💬

NLP

Sentiment analysis, translation, summarisation, chatbots. Modern LLMs (GPT, Claude, Llama) are transformer-based and accessible via APIs.

👁️

Computer Vision

Image classification, object detection, quality inspection. CNNs and vision transformers power face ID, autonomous driving, factory inspection.

🎯 Decision Framework:

For

structured/tabular business data

(majority of enterprise ML) → classical ML wins. For

unstructured data

(images, text, audio) → deep learning. Don't use a neural network when a decision tree will do.

Data Type

Recommended Approach

Why

Tabular (spreadsheet-style)

XGBoost, Random Forest, Logistic Regression

Fast, interpretable, less data needed

Text / Language

Fine-tuned LLMs, BERT, classical NLP

Pre-trained models capture language nuance

Images

CNNs, Vision Transformers (fine-tuned)

Hierarchical spatial feature learning

Business Problem

Do you have labelled data?

Yes

Supervised

No

Unsupervised

Category or number?

Cat.

Num.

LogReg · DT · RF · SVM · XGBoost

LinReg · Ridge · Tree · XGBoost

Find groups or

reduce dimensions?

Groups

Dim.

K-Means · DBSCAN · Hier.

PCA

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

42 / 48

Applied Case Study

End-to-End Case Study: Customer Churn at a European Bank

1

Problem: €5M lost/year to churn

Goal: Predict 30-day churn risk per customer

2

Data: 200K records, 18 months history

Transactions, login freq, support calls, demographics

3

Preprocessing: 12% missing values handled

SMOTE oversampling (churn rate = 8%), feature engineering

4

XGBoost wins cross-validation

Precision: 79%, Recall: 82%, AUC-ROC: 0.91

5

Deployment: Real-time API scoring

Top 2,000 at-risk customers flagged weekly for RMs

6

Result: 34% churn reduction in 6 months

€1.7M saved Year 1 · ROI: 560%

✅ What Worked

Starting with business KPI. SHAP explanations enabled RM conversations: "Here's why we flagged this customer."

❌ What Went Wrong

Underestimated data cleaning time (3 weeks vs 3 days). Initial 400ms latency too slow for real-time. Batch scoring was the fix.

💡 The Human Factor

Relationship managers were skeptical of "AI telling them who to call." Weekly calibration sessions and visible wins converted them into champions.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

44 / 48

Practical ML

Data-Centric vs Model-Centric AI

Andrew Ng's paradigm shift: improve the data, not just the model

Model-Centric (Traditional)

Fix the data → hold it constant → try 50 algorithms and hyperparameter combos → hope for improvement.

Weeks of tuning for marginal gains

The bottleneck is often the data, not the model

Ignores inconsistent or ambiguous labels

Data-Centric (Modern)

Fix the model → hold it constant → systematically improve data quality, consistency, and coverage.

Find and fix inconsistent labels

Identify where model underperforms → add targeted data

Clean & augment data rather than tune endlessly

Often produces bigger gains than model changes

Takeaway:

Improving data quality by 10% typically yields more ROI than increasing model complexity by 10%.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

Building ML in Business

Building an ML Team — Roles & Responsibilities

📊

Data Analyst

Insights, dashboards, KPIs. Bridge to business teams.

🔬

Data Scientist

Builds/evaluates models, runs experiments, communicates results.

⚙️

ML Engineer

Deploys models, builds pipelines, ensures reliability at scale.

🏗️

Data Engineer

Builds data infrastructure, warehouses, pipelines.

🎯

AI Product Manager

Defines problem, owns roadmap, connects business and tech.

For Business Leaders:

Your role is to: (1) clearly define the business problem, (2) ensure data availability, (3) translate model outputs into decisions, and (4) champion cross-functional collaboration.

Company Stage

Recommended Team

Starting Point

Early Startup

1 Data Scientist + 1 Engineer

One high-value use case. Buy vs build.

Scale-up

Small pod (DS + DE + PM + Analyst)

Platform thinking. Data catalogue. Model registry.

Enterprise

Centre of Excellence + embedded teams

Governance, standards, MLOps maturity model.

46 / 48

Looking Ahead

The Future of Machine Learning in Business

Trends shaping the next five years

Emerging Trends

🤖

AutoML

— Automated model selection and tuning. Democratises ML for non-specialists.

📋

Foundation Models & LLMs

— General-purpose models fine-tuned for enterprise tasks. Reducing time-to-value dramatically.

🔒

Privacy-Preserving ML

— Federated learning, differential privacy for regulated data.

📊

Real-Time ML

— Streaming feature stores, decisions in <100ms at scale.

🌍

Responsible AI Regulation

— EU AI Act, ISO 42001. Compliance becomes a competitive moat.

Strategic Priority

By 2027, companies without ML capabilities in core processes will face structural competitive disadvantage in cost, speed, and personalisation.

Talent is the Bottleneck

The shortage of ML talent is severe. Invest in upskilling existing teams and building ML literacy across the organisation.

Data is the Sustainable Asset

Algorithms are commoditised. The company with better, cleaner, and richer proprietary data wins long-term.

CODE · ML FUNDAMENTALS

47 / 48

Course Summary

Key Takeaways

🏗️ Foundation

ML ≠ magic. Quality data beats clever algorithms every time.

📐 Supervised Learning

Start simple. Add complexity only when justified. Always validate out-of-sample.

🔍 Unsupervised

Clustering reveals hidden segments. PCA compresses noise. Market basket unlocks cross-sell.

⚖️ Responsibility

Bias is real, consequences are legal. Explainability is a business requirement.

💰 Business Impact

Quantify ROI before building. The business question matters more than the algorithm.

🚀 Next Steps

Pick one real business problem. Get the data. Run a simple model. Measure. Iterate. Ship.

const TOTAL = 48;

let cur = 1;

function show(n) {

document.querySelectorAll('.slide').forEach(s => s.classList.remove('active'));

document.getElementById('s' + n).classList.add('active');

document.getElementById('counter').textContent = n + ' / ' + TOTAL;

document.getElementById('btnPrev').disabled = n === 1;

document.getElementById('btnNext').disabled = n === TOTAL;

cur = n;

}

function next() { if (cur < TOTAL) show(cur + 1); }

function prev() { if (cur > 1) show(cur - 1); }

document.addEventListener('keydown', e => {

if (e.key === 'ArrowRight' || e.key === 'ArrowDown') next();

if (e.key === 'ArrowLeft' || e.key === 'ArrowUp') prev();

});

show(1);

💡 The Central Metaphor

We didn't

program

data

learn

— just like a child learns to recognise a cat not from a rule-book, but from seeing thousands of cats.

— backprop, transformers, attention

exactly

CODE · NEURAL NETWORKS & DEEP LEARNING

3 / 35

00

Why Neural Networks?

The evolution from statistics to deep learning

Understanding where neural networks come from — and why classical methods weren't enough.

Linear Regression

KNN

SVM

Decision Trees

Why DL wins

Evolution · Classical ML

The Statistics-to-ML Timeline

Every modern AI algorithm has roots in statistics from decades ago

📐

1800s — Linear Regression

Gauss & Legendre. Fit a line to data. Predict house prices, crop yields. Still used daily in business. Works great on

linear

problems.

🔷

1960s — KNN & Logistic Regression

KNN: "find the k most similar examples". Logistic: predict probability of a class. Simple, interpretable, still taught in every MBA.

🌳

1980s–90s — Decision Trees & SVMs

Decision Trees: if-then rules the computer learns. SVMs: find the best boundary between classes. XGBoost (ensemble of trees) still dominates tabular data in 2024.

🧠

1986–2012 — Early Neural Networks

Backprop invented (Rumelhart). Perceptrons gain hidden layers. BUT — not enough data, not enough compute. "AI winter" strikes.

🚀

2012–Now — Deep Learning Explosion

AlexNet wins ImageNet by a landslide using GPU-trained deep CNN. Transformers arrive 2017. ChatGPT 2022. We are here.

🤔 So why not just use Linear Regression?

Linear regression predicts:

y = w·x + b

What if the relationship is

not

a line? What if predicting tumour type from 512×512 pixel MRI scans? That's 262,144 input features with non-linear interactions.

Classical models break. Deep learning thrives.

⚠️ Key Limitation of Classical ML

Feature engineering

— a human expert must manually tell the model which features matter. For images, text, audio → impossible to do manually at scale.

💡 Deep Learning's superpower:

It learns its own features automatically from raw data.

CODE · NEURAL NETWORKS & DEEP LEARNING

5 / 35

Evolution · Why DL?

Classical ML vs Deep Learning — Head to Head

It's not a competition — it's knowing which tool fits which job

Aspect

Classical ML (KNN, SVM, XGBoost…)

Deep Learning (CNN, Transformer…)

Data needed

Works with 100s–10,000s rows

Usually needs 10,000s–millions

Feature engineering

Manual — domain expert defines features

Automatic — model learns features

Structured/tabular data

✅ Excellent (XGBoost wins Kaggle)

⚠️ Often overkill

Images

❌ Very poor

✅ State of the art

Text / Language

⚠️ Bag-of-words, TF-IDF — limited

✅ Transformers dominate

Time series

✅ Good (ARIMA, XGBoost)

✅ LSTM/Transformers excel on complex sequences

Interpretability

✅ Usually explainable

⚠️ "Black box" — needs XAI tools

Compute cost

✅ CPU, seconds

⚠️ GPU, hours/days to train

Performance ceiling

📉 Plateaus with more data

📈 Keeps improving with more data

📈 The Scaling Insight

Classical ML: performance plateaus no matter how much data you add.

Deep Learning: performance

keeps rising

as data and compute grow. This is why Google, Meta, OpenAI keep making models bigger.

🎯 Decision Rule

Tabular data, small dataset

→ XGBoost first

Images / Video / Audio

→ Deep Learning

Text / Language

→ Transformers / LLMs

Time series

→ Try both

CODE · NEURAL NETWORKS & DEEP LEARNING

6 / 35

Data Types

Different Data → Different AI Brains

The architecture you choose is driven by the nature of your data

🖼️

Images

Pixels arranged in a 2D grid. Spatial relationships matter. Cat in corner = cat in center = same cat.

CNN

ViT

🎬

Video

Images across time. Spatial + temporal patterns. Very data hungry. 30fps × hours = enormous.

3D-CNN

Vision Transformer

🗣️

Audio / Voice

Sound wave → spectrogram (image-like). Order and timing are crucial.

CNN on spectrogram

Whisper

📝

Text / Language

Sequences of tokens. Meaning depends on context. "bank" = river or finance?

Transformer

BERT/GPT

📊

Tabular / Structured

Rows and columns. Sales data, medical records. Relationships less spatial.

XGBoost first

TabNet (DL)

🖼️ How Much Data for Images?

From scratch: 100K+ images per class. With transfer learning: as few as

200–500 images

per class can work well.

📝 How Much Data for Text?

Fine-tuning an LLM:

500–5,000 examples

can achieve expert-level performance. Pre-training from scratch: billions of tokens.

🎬 How Much Data for Video?

Very expensive to label. Transfer from image models helps. Production systems use

millions of clips

— hence YouTube/TikTok advantage.

CODE · NEURAL NETWORKS & DEEP LEARNING

7 / 35

01

What is a Neural Network?

Inspired by the brain — built from math

Neurons, layers, weights, activation functions. The building blocks of every AI model.

Neurons

Layers

Weights

Activation Functions

Perceptrons

Foundations

Biological vs Artificial Neurons

The brain has ~86 billion neurons. A deep learning model has millions of artificial ones.

Biological Neuron

Nucleus

Dendrites

(Inputs)

Axon

Synapses

(Outputs)

Receives signals via

dendrites

→ processes in nucleus → fires output along

axon

if signal is strong enough. This

"fire or not"

principle is the direct inspiration for artificial neurons.

Artificial Neuron (Perceptron)

x₁ = 0.8

x₂ = 0.3

x₃ = 0.5

w₁=0.6

w₂=0.9

w₃=0.2

Σ then

activate

0.82

Inputs × Weights → Sum → Activation → Output

Each input is multiplied by a

weight

(the synapse strength), summed, then passed through an

activation function

. The weights are exactly what the network

learns

during training.

CODE · NEURAL NETWORKS & DEEP LEARNING

9 / 35

Foundations

Activation Functions — The Spark of Intelligence

Without them, a neural network is just a fancy linear equation — useless for complex problems

A neuron's raw sum can be any number — activation

shapes

the output

Non-linearity

lets networks learn curves, patterns, and complex boundaries

This is what separates deep learning from linear regression!

ReLU — Rectified Linear Unit

Output = max(0, x). Negative → 0, positive → keeps value.

Most popular

for hidden layers. Fast to compute. The default choice.

Sigmoid

Squashes output to (0,1). Perfect for

binary classification

output — "70% probability it's a cat".

Softmax

Converts outputs to probabilities summing to 1. Used in

multi-class output layers

(e.g. cat 60%, dog 30%, bird 10%).

GELU / SiLU

Smooth versions of ReLU. Used in modern Transformers (GPT, BERT). Slightly better performance in large models.

ReLU

Sigmoid

Tanh

GELU

input x →

output

CODE · NEURAL NETWORKS & DEEP LEARNING

10 / 35

Foundations

Layers & Network Architecture

Stack neurons into layers — stack layers into a "deep" network

Input Layer

x₁

x₂

x₃

x₄

Hidden Layer 1

Hidden Layer 2

Output Layer

Cat

Dog

Every neuron connects to every neuron in the next layer → "Fully Connected" or "Dense" layer

Input layer

— raw data (pixels, numbers, word embeddings)

Hidden layers

— learn increasingly abstract features

Output layer

— final prediction or class probabilities

Deep

= more than one hidden layer

📌 The Layer Abstraction Analogy

For an image of a face:

Layer 1 → detects edges

Layer 2 → combines edges into eyes, noses

Layer 3 → combines features into face structure

Layer 4 → "Is this person happy?"

💡 "Deep"

in Deep Learning refers to the

number of layers

, not the complexity of the math. More layers = more abstract understanding.

CODE · NEURAL NETWORKS & DEEP LEARNING

11 / 35

02

Training a Neural Network

How does a machine actually learn?

Loss functions, backpropagation, gradient descent, optimisers, regularisation.

Loss Functions

Backpropagation

Gradient Descent

Adam

Regularisation

Training

The Loss Function — How Wrong Are We?

Training = minimising the gap between predictions and reality

What is a Loss Function?

A number that measures how

wrong

the model's prediction is. The entire goal of training is to make this number as small as possible.

🎯 Perfect prediction → Loss = 0

❌ Very wrong prediction → Loss is high

Common Loss Functions

Mean Squared Error (MSE) — Regression

Average of squared differences between predicted and actual values. Heavily penalises big errors. Use for: house price prediction.

Cross-Entropy Loss — Classification

Measures how far predicted probabilities are from true labels. Standard for classification. Use for: image recognition, spam detection.

Intuition: Golf Analogy

⛳ Hole (True Label)

Ball A

Loss = HIGH

Ball B

Loss = low

📌 Business Meaning

Loss is your model's error signal. Reducing it on training data is the entire point of learning — but beware reducing it

only

on training data (overfitting).

CODE · NEURAL NETWORKS & DEEP LEARNING

13 / 35

Training

Backpropagation — The Learning Engine

The algorithm that adjusts every weight to reduce the loss

1

Forward Pass

Feed data through the network, get a prediction.

2

Calculate Loss

Compare prediction to correct answer. Measure the error.

3

Backward Pass (Backprop)

Use calculus (chain rule) to calculate how much each weight contributed to the error — propagate backward through every layer.

4

Update Weights

Nudge each weight slightly in the direction that reduces loss (gradient descent).

5

Repeat for thousands of batches

Until loss converges. One full pass through all data = one "epoch".

Gradient Descent — The Mountain Analogy

Weight value →

Loss

Start

Minimum ✓

Analogy:

You're blindfolded on a hilly landscape. You feel the slope under your feet and take a small step downhill. Repeat until you reach the valley — the lowest loss = best weights.

CODE · NEURAL NETWORKS & DEEP LEARNING

14 / 35

Training

Optimisers & Regularisation

Train faster, generalise better

Optimisers — Smarter Descent

SGD — Stochastic Gradient Descent

Updates weights after each batch. Noisy but fast. The classic baseline.

⭐ Adam — Adaptive Moment Estimation

Adapts the learning rate per parameter.

Default choice for 90% of projects.

Start here, always.

Learning Rate — The Step Size

Too large → overshoot. Too small → takes forever.

Rule: start Adam at 0.001.

Regularisation — Prevent Memorisation

⚠️ Overfitting

Training accuracy: 99% → Test accuracy: 62%. The model memorised training data instead of learning patterns.

Dropout

Randomly "switches off" % of neurons during training. Forces redundant, robust representations. Most effective DL regulariser.

Early Stopping + Batch Norm

Stop when validation loss starts rising (early stopping). Normalise layer inputs for stable, faster training (batch norm).

Golden Rule:

Always use Dropout + Early Stopping. They're free and almost always help.
